        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2265600291" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4177997268415194442">This is my first Ashwin Sanghi. I always see his books at bookstores but somehow the idea of mixing a thriller with mythology didn't appeal to me. This one was the best book available at the book swap space at a lit fest and so it was like it had to come to me. <br/> To be honest, I wasn't very disappointed. Like many others have pointed out it is a lot like The DaVinci code. Substitute Hindu mythology for Christian mythology and a professor of history + a sidekick on the run across North India chasi</span>
  <span id="freeText4177997268415194442" style="display:none">This is my first Ashwin Sanghi. I always see his books at bookstores but somehow the idea of mixing a thriller with mythology didn't appeal to me. This one was the best book available at the book swap space at a lit fest and so it was like it had to come to me. <br /> To be honest, I wasn't very disappointed. Like many others have pointed out it is a lot like The DaVinci code. Substitute Hindu mythology for Christian mythology and a professor of history + a sidekick on the run across North India chasing an invaluable artifact - you pretty much have this. It is actually a lot of historical details with a semblance of a plot to carry it to the reader. The amount of information and theories thrown at the reader is impressive. And to know that it was based on solid research makes it even better. of course, at times you feel some of the arguments are juvenile and at times, not so solid. Also, a refresher course on Mahabharata runs parallel to the narrative. I liked it coz I had forgotten most of it :) <br />so, the plot is nothing much to write about but the novel is redeemed by the historical information and theories it throws up.<br />Actual rating: 3.5 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4177997268415194442" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2020351073" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2020351073" />
    <a title="Varunsp" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42118666-varunsp"><img alt="Varunsp" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546678852p2/42118666.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2020351073?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Varunsp" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Varunsp" href="/user/show/42118666-varunsp">Varunsp</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2020351073" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17575561521473229291">This is my second novel by the author. Ofcourse there are similarities between "The Krishna Key" and Dan Brown's "The da Vinci Code", that doesn't mean its a bad read. The author has put a lot of effort in the researching and one can see it throughout the book and there are facts that every Indian should know.<br/>The protagonist is Ravi Mohan Saini, a historian who is accused of murdering his close friend over an ancient artifact which is eventually a part of the mythical "Philosopher's Stone". The </span>
  <span id="freeText17575561521473229291" style="display:none">This is my second novel by the author. Ofcourse there are similarities between "The Krishna Key" and Dan Brown's "The da Vinci Code", that doesn't mean its a bad read. The author has put a lot of effort in the researching and one can see it throughout the book and there are facts that every Indian should know.<br />The protagonist is Ravi Mohan Saini, a historian who is accused of murdering his close friend over an ancient artifact which is eventually a part of the mythical "Philosopher's Stone". The entire story revolves around how Saini can prove his innocence, outwit his enemies, and at the same time solving- What is the legacy that Krishna left behind for us?<br />One thing which I really liked about this book is that in the beginning of every chapter, the story of Krishna is written in a short and concise way.<br />It was really a great learning experience too.<br />I would recommend this book to everyone who like Indian mythology &amp; history .<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17575561521473229291" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_480146014" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/480146014" />
    <a title="Nikhil Harindranath" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15585098-nikhil-harindranath"><img alt="Nikhil Harindranath" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1412072972p2/15585098.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/480146014?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nikhil Harindranath" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nikhil Harindranath" href="/user/show/15585098-nikhil-harindranath">Nikhil Harindranath</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer480146014" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10773615230469653703">Introduction:<br/>This is definitely not the first review of The Krishna Key that you have read. There are thousands out there - All you have to do is to "Google it"! And there is a reason for that - It is Good! But then how good is it depends entirely on you! Let me make it clear right at the start - It is not a book for everyone!<br/><br/>Synopsis: (Spoiler-alert!)<br/>The protagonist is Ravi Mohan Saini, a historian who is accused of murdering his close friend over an ancient artifact which is eventually a part </span>
  <span id="freeText10773615230469653703" style="display:none">Introduction:<br />This is definitely not the first review of The Krishna Key that you have read. There are thousands out there - All you have to do is to "Google it"! And there is a reason for that - It is Good! But then how good is it depends entirely on you! Let me make it clear right at the start - It is not a book for everyone!<br /><br />Synopsis: (Spoiler-alert!)<br />The protagonist is Ravi Mohan Saini, a historian who is accused of murdering his close friend over an ancient artifact which is eventually a part of the mythical "Philosopher's Stone". As expected, apart from the extremely smart cop who is hellbent on capturing him, there is another set of people who would go to any length to get their hands on the same object. The entire story revolves around how Saini can prove his innocence, outwit his enemies, and at the same time solve what has been baffling historians and conspiracy-theorists for centuries - What is the legacy that Krishna left behind for us?<br /><br />The parallel story (much like in Chanakya's Chant) is about Krishna telling his story in his point of view. The author was successful in linking Chanakya's story to that of Gangasagar Mishra in Chanakya's Chant, but failed to do that effectively in The Krishna Key!<br /><br />The Author:<br />Ashwin is a changed man! He had no clue on how to structure the book in The Rozabal Line. This he mastered in Chanakya's Chant. But the numerous jokes and one-liners that he pulled out from email and SMS forwards in Chanakya's Chant made me cringe (although he did give due credit to each at the end of the book). But The Krishna Key is as fresh as Spring! He has set a style of his own and it is awesome. To say he has improved as an author is an understatement. He is Reborn! But despite trying really really hard not to compare, I still could not help thinking that he is highly influenced by Dan Brown.<br /><br />How could it have been Better:<br />At too many places the book points an ugly finger at the beliefs of religions other than Hinduism. Could have been avoided or atleast a bit more subtle. <br />Too much research, too much information - Again, depends on how you feel about it. I loved the book for how much research the author had done, but putting all that into one book was probably not that great an idea.<br />Better proof-reading could have helped. Number of mixups with the names of the characters, facts, etc. He has acknowledged this on his website.<br /><br />Who Will Like It:<br />Do you think that conspiracy theories are fun? Do you, if not love, atleast like a bit of our vedas/puranas, etc.? Do you like fast-paced stories that actually make you wanna flip the pages and find out what happens next? If the answers to these questions are YES, then this is definitely a book for you. Trust me, you will find yourself literally sitting on the edge of the seat as the story reaches the climax. <br /><br />Who Will NOT Like it:<br />This book is a definite no for people who get easily offended on the basis of religion and for people who take things a bit too seriously. Think of it as a story and nothing more. It is fiction. And it is good fiction.<br /><br />My Rating:<br /><br />This is definitely a 3 out of 5:<br /><br />If you thought 3 is too high - I rated it 3 for the sheer dedication that the author has put in to do the required research to make a piece of fiction look like a documentary. And for the effort he has put in to connect all the dots in the end.<br /><br />If you thought 3 is too low - I rated it 3 due to the same reason. The author has tried to put in too much of his research into the book. We are constantly bombarded with numerous research findings and conspiracy theories and he had to rush through everything to make sure he did not leave any loose ends.<br /><br />Summary:<br />The story is fast-paced and interesting, giving a fresh new look at our puranas. The characters are convincingly real. The language is simple enough and yet catchy. Good research has gone into it. And it has a perfect ending. I am really not sure why most of the feedback for this book says that the first half is great and the second half, not so much. I personally feel that there couldn't have been a better ending and the second half is equally thrilling. Ashwin has grown and flourished as an author and the maturity and confidence is evident. Loved it that he has given extensive details about all the places he has referred to (including YouTube video links) as a part of his research.<br /><br />Like I said at the start, this is not a book for everyone. It is for people who love to read just for the love of reading. If you expect too much before you start, you will be disappointed. So assume nothing - Just pick up the book and give it a shot - I am pretty sure you will not be disappointed!<br /><br />Happy Reading!<br /><br /><a href="http://nimmitham.blogspot.in/2012/09/the-krishna-key-book-review.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Read this and more reviews on my Blog</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10773615230469653703" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText8826000893390689953" style="display:none">Till the date of today my favourite book was ANGELS &amp; DEMONS by DAN BROWN but From Now it's Krishna key.<br /><br />The rhetorical tone of analysis, information and thriller fiction go but a little way in describing The Krishna Key. <br /><br />Sanghi, towards the end of his triad makes a splendid job by painting the Indian subcontinent in the light of historical fiction interspersed by archaeological and cryptic analysis. <br /><br />The thriller opens with a gruesome murder and goes on describing a deadly plot, with a fanatic murderer, an observant historian and a pursuant symbologist. <br /><br />In the quest to identify the key, the author takes the reader on a journey across the vast stretches of India and brings forth the unheard analysis of Indus Valley civilization and the impact of Lord Krishna on the generations to follow. <br /><br />The book even explores the depths of the scientific rationale of the contemporary times with the present advancements and paints our forefathers in a new light altogether. <br /><br />If you believe that MAHABHARATA didn't happened then this book you need to read. It will blow your mind away, with with logical &amp; scientific proofs that reveals that it happened Actually.<br /><br />Truly, by the time you reach the end of the pages, you essentially do not want it to end. It grows interesting by the word. Ashwin sir truly knows the key to success and with The Krishna Key he has unlocked the title for himself- India's Dan Brown.</span>

  <span id="freeText6731384950367156746" style="display:none"><b>The Key Seems Lost !!</b><br /><br />Having read his previous books The Rozabal Line and Chanikya's Chant, I did expect a lot from this book hoping to find some conspiracy theories and untold tales about Lord Krishna. But this book turns out to be a slightly less than what I have expected. <br /><br />Ashwin Sanghi's style of developing a plot shares some similarities with Dan Brown's style for which Sanghi is called Dan Brown of India. Nevertheless, Sanghi managed to get readers attention with loads of interesting facts about Indian's, its civilization and the Vedas.At times it was tough separating facts from fiction. The way Sanghi manoeuvred the plot with a meticulous compilation of Mythological, Theological and Scientific facts is splendid.<br /><br />Sanghi tried to arouse curiosity all through the book except the climax which tends to be subtle and the key point of the book is lost amidst the interesting facts and theories.<br /><br /><a href="http://blog.sasidhar.in/2012/08/the-krishna-key-lock-seems-unopened.html" rel="nofollow noopener">More on my blog</a></span>

  <span id="freeText16544591563174650752" style="display:none">I was skeptical as to whether he is trying really hard to become Dan Brown of India. turns out it is true! But I really liked how well he tried to build up the premise and how much research he has done.         <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3419258183" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9187433657280855222">Book Review<br/>The Krishna Key - Ashwin Sanghi<br/>⭐⭐⭐⭐<br/><br/>I loved it! What can I say, Maybe I didn't go in with high expectations. I was longing for something that had a Dan Brown vibe, and this book did not disappoint me at all. <br/>The book production quality, the page quality and print was incredible. I'm so glad I bought the paperback! <br/>First for the good bits - The facts and tidbits in the book around which the entire plot revolves, are very well researched. I got to learn so many new things, including a lo</span>
  <span id="freeText9187433657280855222" style="display:none">Book Review<br />The Krishna Key - Ashwin Sanghi<br />⭐⭐⭐⭐<br /><br />I loved it! What can I say, Maybe I didn't go in with high expectations. I was longing for something that had a Dan Brown vibe, and this book did not disappoint me at all. <br />The book production quality, the page quality and print was incredible. I'm so glad I bought the paperback! <br />First for the good bits - The facts and tidbits in the book around which the entire plot revolves, are very well researched. I got to learn so many new things, including a lot from ancient and medieval history of Hinduism, Krishna and India. <br />The pace of the book is amazing - 480 pages broken down into 3-4 pages chapters, each chapter a new scene - the perfect recipe of a fast paced breezy read. <br />There was never a dull moment till the very last page. <br />I cant believe i finished 485 page book in less than a week! :)<br /><br />Now to the not-so-good bits : <br />The characters were very one dimensional (i kinda expected that from a typical mystery) but they talk in such weird monotones. Nobody in real life talks like that "Can you please take a picture from your blackberry" "can you take out the phone from the pocket of your jeans", "He went to get copies from the duplicating machine." Who the hell talks like that?! Lol :D it's a photocopier! <br />And then the hyphens. Conver-sation, exer-cising, extra-legal (instead of illegal).(so many more), calling desi chinese as hybrid indo-chinese meal :D<br /><br />But inspite of all this, I was not deterred enough to dislike this book. Not every book we read needs to be a literary masterpiece. Some are to be read for sheer reading pleasure and fun!</span>
  <a data-text-id="9187433657280855222" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1524599262" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1524599262" />
    <a title="D.J.Vasudevan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51383534-d-j-vasudevan"><img alt="D.J.Vasudevan" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1524599262?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="D.J.Vasudevan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="D.J.Vasudevan" href="/user/show/51383534-d-j-vasudevan">D.J.Vasudevan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Sreeramprasath R K </span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1524599262" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6432650512990753458">I grabbed this book owing to the genre- Mythology + History = Mystery.<br/><br/>This book speaks Mahabharatham and present day hunt for the secret left over by Lord Krishna in parallel fashion. Starting with Indus valley civilization(Saraswati civilization), the author goes on and on touching the most controversial theories ever heard off regarding Hindu mythology.<br/><br/>Abraham- put the 'A' as last, you will get Brahama<br/><br/>Impressive points:<br/>1.Shiva lingam at Mecca's Kabba- Man! I was literally blown away when the a</span>
  <span id="freeText6432650512990753458" style="display:none">I grabbed this book owing to the genre- Mythology + History = Mystery.<br /><br />This book speaks Mahabharatham and present day hunt for the secret left over by Lord Krishna in parallel fashion. Starting with Indus valley civilization(Saraswati civilization), the author goes on and on touching the most controversial theories ever heard off regarding Hindu mythology.<br /><br />Abraham- put the 'A' as last, you will get Brahama<br /><br />Impressive points:<br />1.Shiva lingam at Mecca's Kabba- Man! I was literally blown away when the author gave comparisons between Hinduism and Islam where it says most following of Islam have emerged from Hinduism. ( The word Allah in arabic language, 786 in ancient hindu symbols resembles OM) <br /><br />2. Taj Mahal: A hindu temple- He says that Taj mahal is actually a palace given by Raja Man Singh of Rajastan to Shah Jahan. A palace and temple co-existed before Taj mahal came into picture. He also lives by his word proving the lotus,coconut mango images in the form of symbols at Taj mahal.<br /><br />3. Shiv = Vish: All through the book, the author never says Vishnu, instead its Vish stating that Shiv and Vish are two sides of the same coin.<br /><br />4. Wonders of somnath temple: The rumors of alchemy(transforming lead to gold) behind the somnath temple pillars. Hanging shiva lingam which is destroyed by Muhhamed ghazini and captured.<br /><br />Plenty of argumentative facts in the form fiction(maybe).<br /><br />Not-so Impressive:<br />1. Kalki- 10th avatar from 21st century: Here , the author failed to take things to next level. (I was disappointed)<br /><br />2.Ceramic silver plate of 4*4cm: This mentioning appears at many chapters. Infact the whole idea of Krishna key was based on the ceramicplate, but again the author failed here atlast. What about the 4 seals as well ?!<br /><br />3. Is Lord Buddha the 9th avatar of Vishnu ?? Comeon! Again conflict of theories, but this didn't hold good.<br /><br />4. Star defined as symbol of life creation: Already this has been said by Dan-brown, Mr.Indian Dan Brown. <br />Too draggy and overloading facts/fiction, though the story keeps every incident gripping with thrill factor engaged.<br /><br />Pick this book if you are a history buff and interested in Hindu mythology. <br />My rating would be 3.5/5 precisely.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6432650512990753458" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1443073610" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1443073610" />
    <a title="Harish Challapalli" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4097355-harish-challapalli"><img alt="Harish Challapalli" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588506276p2/4097355.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1443073610?book_show_action=true">Nov 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Harish Challapalli" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Harish Challapalli" href="/user/show/4097355-harish-challapalli">Harish Challapalli</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">history lovers, fiction lovers,</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Harish by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/20198697-nimal-prashanth">Nimal Prashanth</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4097355-harish-challapalli?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4097355-harish-challapalli?shelf=to-be-made-into-movie">to-be-made-into-movie</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4097355-harish-challapalli?shelf=favorite-authors">favorite-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4097355-harish-challapalli?shelf=indian-authors">indian-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4097355-harish-challapalli?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4097355-harish-challapalli?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4097355-harish-challapalli?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4097355-harish-challapalli?shelf=suspense">suspense</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4097355-harish-challapalli?shelf=ashwin">ashwin</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1443073610" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4451303293299297879">This is the second book of Ashwin Sanghi I read after Chanakya's Chant. I must say I am more than impressed with his interest to write about our history, culture in a more sophisticated and logical way possible. <br/><br/>The Krishna Key is a compilation of many historical incidents, scientific researches with some fiction out of the author's imagination. The extensive research done by the author shows his commitment for giving a perfect output. The entire book goes on with a rapid pace except for the sub</span>
  <span id="freeText4451303293299297879" style="display:none">This is the second book of Ashwin Sanghi I read after Chanakya's Chant. I must say I am more than impressed with his interest to write about our history, culture in a more sophisticated and logical way possible. <br /><br />The Krishna Key is a compilation of many historical incidents, scientific researches with some fiction out of the author's imagination. The extensive research done by the author shows his commitment for giving a perfect output. The entire book goes on with a rapid pace except for the subplot of the lead antagonist. Those chapters made me feel that the author drifted from the plot. Apart from that, every subplot will interest you.<br /><br />At this point, the comparison with the infamous "Davinci Code" is worth mentioning. Yes, we can clearly see many resemblances both the novels have. The author took the former as a theme and befit the later into it. Like the protagonist being a middle aged academician, symbols, codes, ending the story at a monument and etc., all these will definitely come into the readers' mind. Also the way story goes back and forth to mahabharata era and present era will also make the reader compare it with the author's earliest work , Chanakya's Chants. However, the author just confined the mahabarata narration in Lord Krishna's POV to merely a paragraph just before every chapter. <br /><br />One can say, without any qualms that " The Krishna Key" is definitely a knowledge imparting fictitious narration of History. I am glad that i read this , though not a path breaking but, enchanting narrative of Sri Ashwin Sanghi.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4451303293299297879" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ZkXYlE09BgLWB/qSC95jaqaG72oZj64ifuFFbDUNaNecSUP3z4fI42lnaYdxFXG7MEwbGWd6IIfWDZLzhZHClQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4230687.Ashwin_Sanghi">About Ashwin Sanghi</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4230687.Ashwin_Sanghi"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1333093978p3/4230687.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4230687.Ashwin_Sanghi">Ashwin Sanghi
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
2,115
followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4574093849527131255">Ashwin Sanghi—entrepreneur by day, novelist by night—has all the usual qualifications of an Indian businessman. Schooling at the Cathedral & John Connon School, a B.A. (Economics) from St. Xavier’s College, and an M.B.A. (Finance) from the Yale School of Management. Besides being a businessman, Ashwin manages a parallel career as writer of fiction. Ashwin’s first novel, ‘The Rozabal Line’ was orig</span>
  <span id="freeText4574093849527131255" style="display:none">Ashwin Sanghi—entrepreneur by day, novelist by night—has all the usual qualifications of an Indian businessman. Schooling at the Cathedral &amp; John Connon School, a B.A. (Economics) from St. Xavier’s College, and an M.B.A. (Finance) from the Yale School of Management. Besides being a businessman, Ashwin manages a parallel career as writer of fiction. Ashwin’s first novel, ‘The Rozabal Line’ was originally self-published in 2007 under his anagram-pseudonym—Shawn Haigins. The book was subsequently published by Westland in 2008 and 2010 in India under his own name and went on to become a national bestseller. ‘Chanakya’s Chant’ is his second novel in the historical-fiction genre. The book has remained on AC Nielsen’s India Bookscan Top-10 for all of 2011. It won the ‘2010 Crossword-Vodafone Popular Choice Award’ in September 2011. UTV has purchased the movie rights to the book and a film based upon the story is expected soon. Dr. Shashi Tharoor released the novel in Mumbai calling it an “enthralling, delightfully-interesting and gripping read with historical research that is impressive.” The Hindustan Times has called it “a cracker of a page-turner.” Ashwin is currently working on his third novel, as yet untitled, that explores business strategies in ancient India. Ashwin is also working towards a Ph.D. in Creative Writing from Bangor University in Wales. Ashwin lives in Mumbai with his wife, Anushika, and his eight-year old son, Raghuvir.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4574093849527131255" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
